    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

  <span id="freeText16105050785409856575" style="display:none">On a train filled with quietly sleeping passengers, a young man’s life is forever altered when he is miraculously seen by a blind man. In a quiet town an American teacher who has lost her Japanese lover to death begins to lose her own self. On a remote road amid fallow rice fields, four young friends carefully take their own lives—and in that moment they become almost as one. In a small village a disaffected American teenager stranded in a strange land discovers compassion after an encounter with an enigmatic red fox, and in Tokyo a girl named Love learns the deepest lessons about its true meaning from a coma patient lost in dreams of an affair gone wrong.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer259450996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9725465478065988587">This is actually one of my favorite books of all time. Top five for sure. Certainly my favorite opening chapter of all time. Pure poetry from beginning to end (not literally, but you know what I mean). It's not always the most uplifting book ever, but there's real magic to be found in it. And I'm not talking about Harry Potter magic, either. It influenced me to view the world in a whole different way. It strengthened my belief in the human condition. Reminded me how much we all need each other i</span>
  <span id="freeText9725465478065988587" style="display:none">This is actually one of my favorite books of all time. Top five for sure. Certainly my favorite opening chapter of all time. Pure poetry from beginning to end (not literally, but you know what I mean). It's not always the most uplifting book ever, but there's real magic to be found in it. And I'm not talking about Harry Potter magic, either. It influenced me to view the world in a whole different way. It strengthened my belief in the human condition. Reminded me how much we all need each other in this world. It's been too long since I've read it, actually. Many years. I think I'll re-read it and return with a more thorough review that will actually do it justice. But for now, I'll say this... I read this at a time in my life when I wasn't reading or writing much of anything, but this book motivated me to not only start reading a whole lot more, but to sit down and finally take real steps towards publishing my own novel, in hopes that, I too, could sprinkle a handful of magic onto other people's lives, the same way this book does. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9725465478065988587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_69032063" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69032063" />
    <a title="K. Lincoln" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2664606-k-lincoln"><img alt="K. Lincoln" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540477668p2/2664606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69032063?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K. Lincoln" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K. Lincoln" href="/user/show/2664606-k-lincoln">K. Lincoln</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69032063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12303699024511984726">Christopher Barzak is a writer from Ohio who went to Japan. (sound familiar? I actually met him there and had a conversation with him and Yoshio Kobayashi).<br/><br/>And his second novel, The Love We Share Without Knowing , is a book I recommend to Everyone (including my parental units.)<br/><br/>It's the intertwining story, centered in Ami, Japan, of a group of people and how love and death touches their lives.<br/><br/>But the reason I recommend this book, is because there are very few Western authors who can write about J</span>
  <span id="freeText12303699024511984726" style="display:none">Christopher Barzak is a writer from Ohio who went to Japan. (sound familiar? I actually met him there and had a conversation with him and Yoshio Kobayashi).<br /><br />And his second novel, The Love We Share Without Knowing , is a book I recommend to Everyone (including my parental units.)<br /><br />It's the intertwining story, centered in Ami, Japan, of a group of people and how love and death touches their lives.<br /><br />But the reason I recommend this book, is because there are very few Western authors who can write about Japan, and write Japanese characters in Japan, and get it mostly right. I think Christopher Barzak gets it mostly right. He captures the pressures of society so well that when characters commit suicide you understand why.<br /><br />He captures in his foreigners-in-Japan characters some of the reasons we run away from home.<br /><br />And in the end, he teaches through all his characters that we can choose the masks that we wear to tell the world who we are, but in the end, they are only masks and are changeable.<br /><br />It's a beautifully written book as well as being insightful into how English-speaking Westerners and Japanese encounter eachother in Japan.<br /><br />Recommended for: Anyone over the age of 13. (for some truthful scenes about death and sex)</span>
  <a data-text-id="12303699024511984726" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13047918443167896372">This book is hauntingly, achingly beautiful. So much of the fantasy genre is dedicated to escapism, but this book doesn't make me want to escape. It makes me want to run out and hop on a plane to halfway around the word so I can kiss a stranger in a city I've never been to before. It makes me want to embrace life in all its wonderful and terrible ways.</span>

<div id="review_92851994" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/92851994" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1674746-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1384140865p2/1674746.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/92851994?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/1674746-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer92851994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1377974687760762318">"We live in a world of illusion. I'm telling you this up front because I don't want you thinking this story is going to have a happy ending. It won't make any sense out of sadness. It won't redeem humanity in even a small sort of way."<br/><br/>Such begins Christopher Barzak's second novel, and it could describe his philosophy on writing. He seems obsessed with death and dead characters, imagining a world that few of us even want to think about. Add to that the fact that these are short stories, connected</span>
  <span id="freeText1377974687760762318" style="display:none">"We live in a world of illusion. I'm telling you this up front because I don't want you thinking this story is going to have a happy ending. It won't make any sense out of sadness. It won't redeem humanity in even a small sort of way."<br /><br />Such begins Christopher Barzak's second novel, and it could describe his philosophy on writing. He seems obsessed with death and dead characters, imagining a world that few of us even want to think about. Add to that the fact that these are short stories, connected by characters, and I'm not a big fan of short stories.<br /><br />So you'd think it would be a bust, right? I could NOT put this book down - it's a laundry-ignoring, call in sick kinda book. His first book "One for Sorrow" was also very strange, and yet I could not stop reading. I like this one oh, so much better, though. <br /><br />Set in Japan, in a rural area outside of Tokyo, we get a series of stories. Some character from a previous story always shows up in the next one in the cleverest way. The writing is haunting, beautiful. A travel guide to Japan, a coming of age story, and ultimately a story about life and death. Do read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1377974687760762318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7416346-gerhard?shelf=short-fiction">short-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7416346-gerhard?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7416346-gerhard?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7416346-gerhard?shelf=gay-interest">gay-interest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer821474922" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4202098566125825262">There is a particular kind of science fiction that delves into the mysteries of ‘otherness’, such as the works of Ursula K. Le Guin. <i>The Love We Share Without Knowing</i> reminds me of such Le Guin masterworks as <i>Always Coming Home</i>, as both deal with the rituals of alien cultures. Then again, Christopher Barzak also reminds me strongly of William Gibson and David Mitchell, but also particularly of Graham Joyce and Jonathan Carroll.<br/><br/>There is such a plethora of authors and books available today that we</span>
  <span id="freeText4202098566125825262" style="display:none">There is a particular kind of science fiction that delves into the mysteries of ‘otherness’, such as the works of Ursula K. Le Guin. <i>The Love We Share Without Knowing</i> reminds me of such Le Guin masterworks as <i>Always Coming Home</i>, as both deal with the rituals of alien cultures. Then again, Christopher Barzak also reminds me strongly of William Gibson and David Mitchell, but also particularly of Graham Joyce and Jonathan Carroll.<br /><br />There is such a plethora of authors and books available today that we inevitably compare books we like to what we have encountered before. I generally do not have a problem with this, as one’s taste and aesthetic are constantly evolving (one hopes). The fact that Barzak reminds me so strongly of so many other authors I admire is a strong testament to his craft.<br /><br />At the end of the day, of course, he is his own wordsmith. And there is such a singular talent behind this book, such a luminosity of insight and vision, that it is quite overpowering and overwhelming at times.<br /><br />What I loved about this book is how unclassifiable it is, how much of a shimmering mystery it refracts into when one reads it. This intricate Russian Doll nesting of short stories, linked together by a careful patterning of repeat characters, is breathtaking to behold as a work of literary art.<br /><br />I cannot begin to say enough about Barzak’s technical mastery here. Suffice it to say, this novel is simply exquisite. It takes a while to get into, and at the beginning all the pieces seem so random and ephemeral, but then when things start to fit together, there is such a sense of resolute completion and joyous culmination, that the reader is quite swept away.<br /><br />No novel can be complete without heart and emotion though. This is one of the best evocations of expatriate culture I have ever read, and of being a foreigner on strange shores. Expats know innately how such a profound dislocation can often highlight the idiosyncrasies of their own societies as much as it does of their host cultures; Barzak explores this dichotomy with intimacy and delicacy.<br /><br />This book caused such conflicting emotions in me. It is sad and funny, melancholic and elegiac, tender and wistful. In other words, full of the wondrous dichotomies of life. Barzak’s focus on Japanese culture, and its curious, almost schizophrenic commingling of folk tradition and cutting-edge technology and cultural assimilation, is a very good metaphor for the dislocation that the modern, hyper-texted world often engenders in its cosmopolitan masses.<br /><br />How to deal with loneliness and being alone in an urban sprawl, the effort it takes to connect with a fellow human being, and the poverty of feeling and reaction as we become increasingly benumbed by the very technology that we think is our only salvation: this novel is an extraordinary paean to what it means to be human.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4202098566125825262" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1243294106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1243294106" />
    <a title="Joe Mccarthy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29624361-joe-mccarthy"><img alt="Joe Mccarthy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1243294106?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Mccarthy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Mccarthy" href="/user/show/29624361-joe-mccarthy">Joe Mccarthy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1243294106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3709621243984099242">If at first the haphazard sections in Christopher Barzak's "The Love We Share Without Knowing" throw you off, don't worry -- he'll soon take your hand and explain every theme again and again in as simple a way as possible so you won't have any trouble seeing how they fit together. <br/><br/>Normally I enjoy when authors fragment narratives, but I only appreciated Barzak's decision to splinter his narrative in almost every section because if the individual sections were extended, they would be too dull to </span>
  <span id="freeText3709621243984099242" style="display:none">If at first the haphazard sections in Christopher Barzak's "The Love We Share Without Knowing" throw you off, don't worry -- he'll soon take your hand and explain every theme again and again in as simple a way as possible so you won't have any trouble seeing how they fit together. <br /><br />Normally I enjoy when authors fragment narratives, but I only appreciated Barzak's decision to splinter his narrative in almost every section because if the individual sections were extended, they would be too dull to read. Set in Japan, Barzak tries to handle his characters, especially the Japanese, delicately, but ends up crafting almost entirely vapid, sentimental sequences. Magical realism somehow fails to enliven the book and comes off as the half-hearted gimmick of someone too circumspect to offend a culture. There are some moving passages, but they exist in a world teeming with platitudes. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3709621243984099242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText11967920799251132575" style="display:none">This novel was nominated for both Nebula and Locus awards for best novel. However, it is clearly not a SF and only marginally a fantasy. The book itself states it is a “psychological fiction”. I’d add magical realism.<br />The novel consists of a score of short stories. They are all set in modern Japan and a group of Americans and Japanese. Each story has own main character(s) but the following ones uses earlier ones as a background and/or minor characters. This is a very nice primer to some aspects of Japanese culture and how it is viewed by English-language teachers from the US. The list of topics ranges from suicide to homosexuality to inability to let go to, as the title suggests ‘The Love We Share Without Knowing’. It is a very nice literary fiction, albeit not the type I usually read.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1531486286" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1531486286" />
    <a title="Jefferson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29294788-jefferson"><img alt="Jefferson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436392574p2/29294788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1531486286?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jefferson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jefferson" href="/user/show/29294788-jefferson">Jefferson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29294788-jefferson?shelf=book-club-books">book-club-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1531486286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6857064806566132608">I could be flip and say I hated this book, and that wouldn't be too far from the truth. I read it for a book club, otherwise I would have put it down after a few chapters. I'm glad I stuck it out, though, as the last two chapters were actually pretty good, and I also realized a little bit more about the structure of the novel as a whole. There is some craft here, some intent to the writing. <br/><br/><i>The Love We Share Without Knowing</i> (great title, by the way) is a series of interlocking stories set in Jap</span>
  <span id="freeText6857064806566132608" style="display:none">I could be flip and say I hated this book, and that wouldn't be too far from the truth. I read it for a book club, otherwise I would have put it down after a few chapters. I'm glad I stuck it out, though, as the last two chapters were actually pretty good, and I also realized a little bit more about the structure of the novel as a whole. There is some craft here, some intent to the writing. <br /><br /><i>The Love We Share Without Knowing</i> (great title, by the way) is a series of interlocking stories set in Japan. Each chapter has a different point of view: an American teenager whose family has moved to Japan, a dissatisfied secretary who commits suicide, a group of American English teachers, the ghost of a young girl. I realized at the end that the chapters mirror each other, the first and last chapter are a pair, then the second and second to last and so forth, though there are threads that connect all of them. The tone is poetic and philosophical, which works best for the interior monologues of the Japanese characters, and sounds stilted in the mouths of the Americans. In one chapter, where a gay American confronts his mother as they tour Japan, the dialogue was egregiously bad: no one talks like that.<br /><br />I can't fault the author for his ambition, and like I said, there is some craft here. It's not lazy writing. What I kept thinking as I read, though, was that David Mitchell did this exact type of story with more success in <i>Ghostwritten</i> and <i>number9dream</i>. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6857064806566132608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2380763051" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2380763051" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36711648-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2380763051?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/36711648-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2380763051" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10833607842552377982">THE LOVE WE SHARE WITHOUT KNOWING is beautifully told in connected short stories of different characters throughout the book. I loved how each story had a small or big effect with one or two of the other stories. The writing is amazingly stunning at times.<br/><br/>I wasn't expecting this to be separate stories which take place in and around Tokyo and yet fill in the blanks of character's stories. (I will admit that I wanted a story of one protagonist after the first "Chapter" and so was a bit disappointe</span>
  <span id="freeText10833607842552377982" style="display:none">THE LOVE WE SHARE WITHOUT KNOWING is beautifully told in connected short stories of different characters throughout the book. I loved how each story had a small or big effect with one or two of the other stories. The writing is amazingly stunning at times.<br /><br />I wasn't expecting this to be separate stories which take place in and around Tokyo and yet fill in the blanks of character's stories. (I will admit that I wanted a story of one protagonist after the first "Chapter" and so was a bit disappointed because we never got back to that first American family that moved to a quiet town outside of Tokyo.) The other characters connected well, they worked together and interacted together. The Kitsune, Midora, was one of the few connecting character through the majority of the stories--in one way or another. I loved her character and the different reasons for Americans to want to teach English in Japan and how different the cultures were: Who wanted to stay, who wanted to go home. Wanting to find love, finding love, losing love, feeling at home, and feeling foreign all at the same time.<br /><br />The stories woven into a tapestry of two cultures and generational attitudes that created a rich picture that was obviously based on Barzak's time teaching in Japan himself and from that time, these stories were given life.<br /><br />If you love language and storytelling, this is the book for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10833607842552377982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan" href="/user/show/1215485-megan">Megan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1215485-megan?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1215485-megan?shelf=courier">courier</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer91197876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17635943197875897542">This book is filled with so many tiny little beautiful lines, hidden in sad paragraphs. It's hard not to feel depressed after reading this, even though there are bright spots of hope every once in awhile. Every story in this book is interconnected, in the same way a movie like <i>Love, Actually</i> has multiple characters threading in and out of each other's lives. I sometimes found it difficult to remember which character was which, never quite sure what the author was alluding to or what connections </span>
  <span id="freeText17635943197875897542" style="display:none">This book is filled with so many tiny little beautiful lines, hidden in sad paragraphs. It's hard not to feel depressed after reading this, even though there are bright spots of hope every once in awhile. Every story in this book is interconnected, in the same way a movie like <i>Love, Actually</i> has multiple characters threading in and out of each other's lives. I sometimes found it difficult to remember which character was which, never quite sure what the author was alluding to or what connections he was trying to make, but that kind of contributed to the overall story. Everyone ends up connected in various ways, even if they themselves never realize it, don't know how the person they brush by on the subway is connected to their own lives.<br /><br />The only difficulty I had with the book besides mixing characters up was the dialogue. It was just hard to read. People don't speak in paragraphs, they don't spill out gobs of text without being interrupted, forced to pause for clarification. It felt jarring and unrealistic for me to have blocks of dialogue the same length as narrative.<br /><br />Other than that, the book was a good read. It's really hard to describe the mood of the book, but I took away from it a little bit of hope amidst a lot of sadness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17635943197875897542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bookmarks Magazine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bookmarks Magazine" href="/user/show/1008236-bookmarks-magazine">Bookmarks Magazine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008236-bookmarks-magazine?shelf=july-aug-2010">july-aug-2010</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer107446319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14739964949123468378">Have we unfairly placed this novel in the SF section? That's where Barzak and his fans come from, but this story will appeal to those who normally don't touch the genre. As far as classification difficulties go, many critics felt it was a stretch to call <em>The Love We Share Without Knowing</em> a novel rather than a short story collection. But few held this against Barzak, and it was clear that every reviewer fell in love with at least one story from the book. Critics also appreciated Barzak's light fa</span>
  <span id="freeText14739964949123468378" style="display:none">Have we unfairly placed this novel in the SF section? That's where Barzak and his fans come from, but this story will appeal to those who normally don't touch the genre. As far as classification difficulties go, many critics felt it was a stretch to call <em>The Love We Share Without Knowing</em> a novel rather than a short story collection. But few held this against Barzak, and it was clear that every reviewer fell in love with at least one story from the book. Critics also appreciated Barzak's light fantastic touch; they hesitated to even call it "magical realism," since events that seem to have supernatural elements to one character in the book may seem completely pedestrian to another. As several observers pointed out, this is a particularly apt style for the depiction of Japan, a simultaneously traditional and modern country. It also suits the book's young characters, who are caught between a longing for the fantasy of childhood and the independence of adulthood. This is an excerpt from a review published in <a href="http://www.bookmarksmagazine.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Bookmarks magazine</a>.</span>

<div id="review_1575571262" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1575571262" />
    <a title="Brad Medd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10577506-brad-medd"><img alt="Brad Medd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577923602p2/10577506.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1575571262?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad Medd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad Medd" href="/user/show/10577506-brad-medd">Brad Medd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10577506-brad-medd?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1575571262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8325304983603675117">'The Love We Share Without Knowing' is a richly woven series of narratives surrounding communication, love, and the complications of identity created by our own human nature. Barzak's talent for articulating with few words what I've found impossible with many is what sets his work apart from anything else I've read. The range of feeling and emotional depth that hides beneath each sentence is astonishing. For the second time, Barzak's work has been the hand that reaches out to my soul, not only o</span>
  <span id="freeText8325304983603675117" style="display:none">'The Love We Share Without Knowing' is a richly woven series of narratives surrounding communication, love, and the complications of identity created by our own human nature. Barzak's talent for articulating with few words what I've found impossible with many is what sets his work apart from anything else I've read. The range of feeling and emotional depth that hides beneath each sentence is astonishing. For the second time, Barzak's work has been the hand that reaches out to my soul, not only offering clarity but new directions to gaze in when I've grown tired staring down the old ones. <br /><br />This novel is achingly beautiful, even to those who've never set foot in Japan, and Barzak's understanding of Japanese culture fits the novel's ideas perfectly. How can somewhere so far be so familiar? What does if mean to be Japanese? What does if mean to be American? What does it mean to be human, or anything at all?<br /><br />Read it. Even if it gives you no answers, you won't be left feeling alone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8325304983603675117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_422211011" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/422211011" />
    <a title="Jack Hastings" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5227491-jack-hastings"><img alt="Jack Hastings" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304282309p2/5227491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/422211011?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack Hastings" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack Hastings" href="/user/show/5227491-jack-hastings">Jack Hastings</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5227491-jack-hastings?shelf=contemporary-lit">contemporary-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer422211011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4750517111691504088">Yes, there is more than a passing resemblance to Murukami in approach and certainly in setting, but Barzak is more accessible and poetic in prose. There is a tendeness toward his characters, to the human condition and the complications of love, that sets him apart. Highly recommended.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/U7bcSvJ31uf1CezukYg07eRTI81bbRevfMKb8cEHm05lmNIXRQz2Y1lKPtHlSsnR3JhRnqdIWHAjdjCCEq09w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('10ndmAMf+t6pZ31W6MrFKN8BrEkx+gP/kbN122aSycQTkWWhdcIWXLvWch4VGc7cL+KBgH4KlsDszad2qdxjXg==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/396931.Christopher_Barzak">About Christopher Barzak</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/396931.Christopher_Barzak"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1440452205p3/396931.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/396931.Christopher_Barzak">Christopher Barzak
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
388
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16158878640436940102">Christopher Barzak is the author of the Crawford Award winning novel, One for Sorrow, which was made into the Sundance feature film, Jamie Marks is Dead. His second novel, The Love We Share Without Knowing, was a finalist for the Nebula and Tiptree Awards. His third novel, Wonders of the Invisible World, is a Stonewall Honor Book. He is also the author of Before and Afterlives, which won the Shirl</span>
  <span id="freeText16158878640436940102" style="display:none">Christopher Barzak is the author of the Crawford Award winning novel, One for Sorrow, which was made into the Sundance feature film, Jamie Marks is Dead. His second novel, The Love We Share Without Knowing, was a finalist for the Nebula and Tiptree Awards. His third novel, Wonders of the Invisible World, is a Stonewall Honor Book. He is also the author of Before and Afterlives, which won the Shirley Jackson Award for Best Collection. His most current novel is, The Gone Away Place. He grew up in rural Ohio, has lived in a southern California beach town, the capital of Michigan, and has taught English outside of Tokyo, Japan. Currently he teaches fiction writing in the Northeast Ohio MFA program at Youngstown State University.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16158878640436940102" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
